Now, the sequence a_n is defined by a_{n+1}=T(a_n). For the sequence to be infinite, we need each a_n to have at least three proper divisors. Thus we need a systematic characterization.

Given the recurrence, we can try to analyze the behavior in terms of the prime factorization. Let's denote N. Write its prime factorization. We need to find all N such that the orbit never hits a number with <3 proper divisors.

Observation: If N is a fixed point (multiple of 6, not divisible by 4 or 5), then it works.

Now, what about numbers that map to a fixed point? For such numbers, the orbit will become constant after one step. So they are good if they themselves have at least three proper divisors. So we need to find all even N such that T(N) is a fixed point (i.e., multiple of 6, not divisible by 4 or 5). And also N itself must have at least three proper divisors. So the set of good numbers includes all numbers that eventually reach a fixed point. Could there be numbers that map to a fixed point after more than one step? That would be numbers that map to a good number that is not fixed, but eventually becomes fixed. So the set of good numbers is the preimage of the set of fixed points under T, and also the preimage of those preimages, etc. In other words, it is the set of numbers that are in the "basin of attraction" of the fixed points. So we need to find the entire set of numbers that eventually land on a fixed point.

Now, could there be numbers that never land on a fixed point but still stay in the set? Possibly cycles of length >1. But we haven't found any. Let's try to see if a cycle could exist. Suppose we have a cycle. Since T(N) = N/d1+N/d2+N/d3, and d1,d2,d3 are divisors, we have that T(N) is at least N/2+? Actually for even N, d1=2, so T(N)=N/2+N/d2+N/d3. Since d2,d3≥2, we have T(N) ≤ N/2+N/2+N/2 = 3N/2. But also, if N is not divisible by 2? Already even. So T(N) could be less than N if N/d2+N/d3 < N/2. For odd N, T(N) < N. So the sequence is strictly decreasing until it becomes even? Actually for even N, T(N) could be less than N, equal to N, or greater than N. For example, N=12 gives T=13 >12; N=18 gives T=18; N=20 gives T=19 <20; N=24 gives T=26 >24; N=30 gives T=31 >30; N=36 gives T=39 >36; N=40 gives T=38 <40; N=42 gives T=42; N=44 gives T=37 <44; N=48 gives T=52 >48; N=50 gives T=40 <50; N=52 gives T=43 <52; N=54 gives T=54; N=56 gives T=50 <56; N=60 gives T=65 >60; N=62 gives T=34 <62; N=64 gives T=56 >64? Maybe we need to consider the transformation in terms of the exponent of 2. Let's analyze the structure of numbers that are good.

We have fixed points: numbers N that are multiples of 6, not divisible by 4, and not divisible by 5. That is, N = 2 * 3^k * m, where m is coprime to 2 and 3, and m is not divisible by 5. Also need N > 6? Actually N=6 works, 6=2*3, so k=1,m=1. So these are fixed points.

Now, numbers that map to a fixed point: T(N) = N/2+N/d2+N/d3 is a fixed point. So we need to solve for N even such that T(N) is a fixed point. This is a condition that might be satisfied by numbers of the form 2 * (some numbers) with specific properties.

From our computed examples: N=72 -> T=78 (fixed). N=216 -> T=234 (fixed). N=504 -> T=546 (fixed). So these are all multiples of 6? 72=6*12, 216=6*36, 504=6*84. We can try to analyze the recursion by considering the exponent of 2 and the odd part. Let's define N. Write N = 2^e * m, with m odd.

We need to compute d1,d2,d3, the three smallest divisors >1. Since 2 is a divisor, d1=2. Then d2: the next smallest divisor. If e≥2, then 4 is a divisor. So the second smallest divisor is min(3,4) if both divide N, else the smallest odd prime factor if N not divisible by 3, else 3. So we need to consider cases based on whether N is divisible by 3 and whether e≥2.

Case 1: N is not divisible by 3. Then the smallest odd prime factor p (p≥5). Since N is even, d2 = p if p ≤ 4? But p≥5, so d2 = p. And d3: the next smallest divisor. It could be 4 if e≥2, or p^2 if p^2 divides N, or maybe 2p? Actually the divisors in increasing order: 2, then p, then either 4 (if e≥2) or 2p? Since p is odd, 2p is even and may be less than p^2 if p>2? For p=5, 2p=10, p^2=25, so 10 < 25. But is 2p a divisor? Yes, because 2 divides N and p divides N, so 2p divides N. So after 2 and p, the next smallest divisor could be 4 if e≥2 and 4 < 2p? Compare 4 and 2p: for p≥5, 2p≥10, so 4 < 2p. So if e≥2, then 4 is a divisor and 4 < 2p. So d3 = 4. If e=1, then 4 is not a divisor, and the next divisor after 2 and p is 2p? Since p^2 > 2p for p>2? For p=5, p^2=25, 2p=10, so 2p < p^2. So d3 = 2p. But also there might be a composite divisor like p*q? But q > p, so 2p is smaller. So d3 = 2p. So we have two subcases: e≥2 and e=1.

Case 2: N is divisible by 3. Then d2=3. Then d3: the next smallest divisor. It could be 4 if e≥2, or 6? Actually after 2 and 3, the next smallest divisor could be 4 (if e≥2) or 6 (since 6 divides N). Compare 4 and 6: 4 < 6. So if e≥2, then d3=4. If e=1, then 4 is not a divisor, and the next smallest divisor is 6 (since 6=2*3 divides N). So d3=6. But also there might be other divisors like 8? But 8 would be larger than 6 if e≥3? Actually if e≥3, then 8 is a divisor, but compare 8 and 6: 6 < 8, so d3 is still 4 if e≥2? Wait we need the third smallest divisor >1. So the sequence of divisors in increasing order: 2,3,... Then the next candidate is 4 (if e≥2) because 4 < 6. So d3=4. For e=1, the next is 6 because 6 < other divisors like 9? But 9 might be divisor if 9|N, but 6 < 9. So d3=6.

Thus in all cases, we can determine d1,d2,d3 based on e and whether 3|N.

Now, T(N)=N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3.

We can then analyze the parity and divisibility by 5 and 3.

Our goal: Find all N such that the sequence never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. We have already seen that if N is a fixed point (i.e., T(N)=N), then it works. Also if N maps to a fixed point, then it works. But are there numbers that map to numbers that are not fixed but eventually become fixed? Yes, like 72 maps to 78 (fixed). So these are preimages of fixed points. But also there might be numbers that map to 72 (which is a preimage of fixed point), and then 72 maps to fixed, so they also work. So the set of good numbers is the preimage of the set of fixed points under iterates of T. So we need to find all numbers that are eventually in the set of fixed points.

Thus the problem reduces to: Determine all positive integers N such that the orbit under T eventually reaches a fixed point (i.e., a number N with T(N)=N and at least three proper divisors). And we need to find all such N that also have at least three proper divisors (since a1 must have at least three proper divisors). But note that if N is in the basin of attraction of a fixed point, then by definition it eventually reaches a fixed point. However, could there be numbers that never reach a fixed point but still stay within the set (e.g., cycles of length >1)? We haven't found any, but we need to prove that no such cycles exist. Possibly we can prove that if N is not a fixed point, then T(N) < N for all N except those with a certain property? But we saw T(N) > N for many numbers. However, maybe we can show that if T(N) > N, then the orbit will eventually decrease to a fixed point. But it's not immediate.

Alternatively, we can try to find a pattern by considering the transformation in terms of the "3 largest proper divisors". Perhaps we can show that the only numbers that are good are those that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5, and also have the property that the exponent of 2 is not 2? But 36 is multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, exponent of 2 = 2, fails. 60 exponent 2 = 2? 60=2^2*3*5, contains 5, so fails the "not divisible by 5" condition. So maybe the condition is: N is a multiple of 6, and N/6 is odd? But 18/6=3 odd, works; 42/6=7 odd, works; 54/6=9 odd, works; 66/6=11 odd, works; 78/6=13 odd, works; 102/6=17 odd, works; 114/6=19 odd, works; 126/6=21 odd, works; 138/6=23 odd, works; 162/6=27 odd, works; 174/6=29 odd, works; 186/6=31 odd, works; 198/6=33 odd, works; 222/6=37 odd, works; 246/6=41 odd, works; 282/6=47 odd, works; 294/6=49 odd, works; 306/6=51 odd, works; 318/6=53 odd, works; 342/6=57 odd, works; 366/6=61 odd, works; 378/6=63 odd, works; 402/6=67 odd, works; 426/6=71 odd, works; 438/6=73 odd, works; 474/6=79 odd, works; 498/6=83 odd, works; 504/6=84 even, so 504 fails? But we found 504 works. So N=504 works, but 504/6=84 is even. So the condition "N/6 odd" is not necessary. So the class of good numbers includes numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 5, and also have a specific exponent of 2 that leads to T(N) being a fixed point. Let's compute N=504: 504/6=84, even. 504=2^3*3^2*7, exponent of 2=3. So perhaps the exponent of 2 must be 1 or 3? Let's check other fixed points: exponent of 2 = 1. So fixed points have exponent 1. The preimages we found have exponent 3. Are there any numbers with exponent 5 that work? For example, N=2^5*3^2? 32*9=288, we tested fails. N=2^5*3? 96 fails. N=2^5*9*? 288 fails. So not. Could there be numbers with exponent 2 that work? We tried many: 12,20,36,44,52,56,68,76,92,100,116,124,140,152,156,164, etc. None worked. So maybe the only good numbers are those with exponent of 2 either 1 or 3, and also with the odd part divisible by 3? For exponent 1, we need odd part divisible by 3 (since fixed points require divisible by 6, so odd part must be multiple of 3). For exponent 3, we also need odd part divisible by 3? For N=72=2^3*9, odd part 9 divisible by 3. For N=216=2^3*27, odd part 27 divisible by 3. For N=504=2^3*63, odd part 63 divisible by 3. For N=648=2^3*81, odd part 81 divisible by 3. For N=1512=2^3*189, odd part 189 divisible by 3. So in all these, the odd part is divisible by 3. But also note that the odd part cannot be divisible by 5? For N=72, odd part 9 not divisible by 5. For N=216, 27 not divisible by 5. For N=504, 63 divisible by 7 but not 5. For N=648, 81 not divisible by 5. For N=1512, 189 not divisible by 5. So condition: odd part not divisible by 5. Also the exponent of 2 is exactly 3? Could there be numbers with exponent 3 that are not divisible by 3? For example, N=2^3*7=56, we tested fails. So the odd part must be a multiple of 3. So N=2^3 * 3 * t, where t is odd and not divisible by 5? But then N=24 * (3t)? Actually 2^3*3=24, so N=24 * (3t)? Wait: If odd part = 3 * t, then N = 2^3 * 3 * t = 24t. So N = 24t, with t odd. But we earlier found that for N=24t, T(N)=26t, and for this to be a fixed point, we need 26t to be fixed point, which requires t divisible by 3. Because 26t must be divisible by 3, so t multiple of 3. So t = 3k, then N = 72k. So indeed N = 72k with k odd and not divisible by 5? Actually t=3k, t odd implies k odd. And 26t=78k must be fixed point, so 78k must be fixed point. 78k is fixed point if 4 ∤ 78k and 5 ∤ 78k and 6|78k. 6|78k always, 4 ∤ 78k because 78k is even but only one factor 2? 78=2*3*13, so 78k has exponent of 2 exactly 1 if k odd, so not divisible by 4. 5 ∤ 78k requires 5 ∤ k. So k odd and not divisible by 5. So N=72k, k odd and not divisible by 5. So the preimage of fixed points under T is the set of numbers N=72k where k is odd and not divisible by 5. But note that these N themselves may have exponent of 2 = 3? For N=72k, if k odd, then N=2^3*3^2*k, so exponent of 2 is exactly 3 (since 72=8*9, 8=2^3). So indeed exponent 3. But could there be numbers with exponent 3 that are not of the form 72k? For example, N=2^3*3*7=168, we tested fails. So only those with odd part divisible by 9? Actually 72k = 2^3 * 3^2 * k, so odd part = 3^2 * k, which is divisible by 9. So the odd part must be divisible by 9. So the condition for a number N to be in the preimage of fixed points is: N is divisible by 8 and by 9, i.e., divisible by 72, and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. That's a class.

Now, what about preimages of these preimages? That is, numbers N such that T(N) = 72k, with k odd and not divisible by 5. Then such N would also be good. So we need to find all N such that T(N) is in the set S = {72k : k odd, 5∤k}. Then we can continue. This suggests that the set of good numbers is the union of all preimages of the fixed point set under iterates of T. In other words, it is the set of numbers that eventually land on a fixed point. So we need to characterize this entire set.

Given the complexity, maybe we can find a characterization by considering the transformation in terms of the "3 largest proper divisors". Now, are there numbers that map to numbers of the form 72k (which are not fixed points) but are not themselves of the form 72k? That is, numbers N such that T(N) = 72k, with k odd and 5∤k. Then N would also be good. So we need to find all N such that T(N) is in the set S = {72k : k odd, 5∤k}. This is the preimage of S. Then we can iterate. So the set of good numbers is the union of all preimages of the fixed point set under iterates of T. This is essentially the set of numbers that eventually reach a fixed point.

Given the complexity, maybe we can prove that the only numbers that are good are those that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5. But we have counterexample: 36 is multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, but fails. So not that.

Maybe we can prove that if a1 is not a fixed point, then the sequence will eventually reach a number that is either prime or prime square, unless a1 is of the form 72k with k odd and 5∤k. Our goal: Find all N such that the sequence never hits a prime or prime square. Since the sequence is defined by T, and if we ever hit a fixed point, we are done. Perhaps we can show that if N is not a fixed point, then T(N) is either less than N or eventually becomes less. But we saw T(N) > N for many numbers. Let's analyze the transformation in terms of the exponent of 2 and the odd part. Perhaps we can show that if a1 is not a fixed point, then the sequence will eventually become a fixed point if and only if a1 is divisible by 6 and not by 5, and also a1 is not in the set of numbers that map to a prime or prime square. But we need to determine all such a1. This seems like a complex classification.

Maybe we can approach by considering the process as "replace N by N/d1+N/d2+N/d3". We can think of the transformation as f(N). For N even, we have f(N)=N/2+N/d2+N/d3. Since d2 and d3 are divisors, we can write N=2^e*m. Then we can compute f(N) in terms of e and m.

Let's do a systematic analysis of cases based on e and whether 3|N.

Let N=2^e*m, m odd, m>1 (if m=1 then N is a power of 2, which fails because it has only one proper divisor? Actually 2^e with e≥2 has proper divisors: 1,2,4,...,2^{e-1}. It has at least three proper divisors if e≥3? For e=3, N=8, proper divisors:1,2,4 -> three, but T(8)=7, which is prime. So powers of 2 fail. For e=4, N=16, proper divisors:1,2,4,8 -> T=8+4+2=14, 14->10->... fails. So all powers of 2 fail. So we can assume m>1.

Now, we need to consider the three smallest divisors.

The divisors in increasing order: 1, d1, d2, d3, ... where d1 is the smallest divisor >1, which is 2 (since N even). Then d2 is the next smallest divisor >1. It could be 3 if 3|N, else it could be 4 if 4|N, else it could be some odd prime p≥5. Since N is even and m>1, there is at least one odd divisor >1. The smallest odd divisor >1 is the smallest odd prime factor of N, call it p. So d2 = min(3, p, 4?) Actually if 3 divides N, then p=3, so d2=3. If 3 does not divide N, then d2 = p if p<4? But p≥5, so d2=p. But also 4 may be a divisor if e≥2. If e≥2, then 4 is a divisor. So when 3∤N, we have two candidates: p and 4. Since p≥5, 4 < p, so the second smallest divisor is 4 if e≥2. If e=1, then 4 is not a divisor, so d2 = p. So we have:

- If 3|N: d2 = 3.
- If 3∤N and e≥2: d2 = 4.
- If 3∤N and e=1: d2 = p (the smallest odd prime factor).

Now, d3: the next smallest divisor after d1 and d2. We need to consider cases.

Case A: 3|N. Then d1=2, d2=3. Now d3: the next smallest divisor could be 4 if e≥2, or 6 if e=1? But also if 5 divides N, then 5 is a divisor; compare 5 and 4? 4<5, so if e≥2, d3=4. If e=1, then 4 not divisor. Then the next smallest divisor after 2 and 3 is either 5 if 5|N, or 6 if 6|N? But 6=2*3, and since N even and 3|N, 6|N. So 6 is a divisor. Compare 5 and 6: 5<6, so if 5|N, then d3=5. If 5∤N, then d3=6. So:
- If e≥2: d3=4.
- If e=1: d3 = min(5,6) if both divide; if 5∤N then d3=6.

Thus for 3|N:
- e≥2: d1=2,d2=3,d3=4.
- e=1: d1=2,d2=3,d3 = 5 if 5|N, else 6.

Case B: 3∤N and e≥2. Then d1=2,d2=4. Now d3: the next smallest divisor after 2 and 4. Candidates: 8 if e≥3, or 3? But 3∤N, so 3 not divisor. The next could be 5 if 5|N, or 6? But 6=2*3, and 3∤N, so 6 not divisor. So after 2 and 4, the next smallest divisor is either 5 (if 5|N) or 8 (if e≥3) or maybe a prime p≥5. Since p≥5, and 5<8, so if 5|N, then d3=5. If 5∤N, then d3 = min(8, next odd prime p) but p≥7, and 8<7? No, 8>7. So if e≥3, then 8 is a divisor, and if there is an odd prime p=7, then 7<8, so d3=7 if 7|N. So d3 is the smallest divisor among: 5,7,8, etc. So we need to consider the smallest odd prime factor p. Since 3∤N, p≥5. So d3 = min(5,8, p) if both 5 and 8 are divisors? But 8 is only if e≥3. So we have subcases based on e and p.

Case C: 3∤N and e=1. Then d1=2,d2=p (smallest odd prime factor). Now d3: the next smallest divisor after 2 and p. Candidates: 2p (since 2 and p divide N, so 2p|N). Also 4? e=1 so 4 not divisor. Also p^2? If p^2 divides N, then p^2 may be larger than 2p for p≥5? 2p=10, p^2=25, so 2p < p^2. So d3 = 2p (since 2p is a divisor). But also if there is another odd prime q>p, then 2q? That would be larger than 2p? 2p=10, 2q≥14, so 2p is smaller. So d3 = 2p. However, if p=5 and also 3 divides N? But 3∤N. So d3=2p.

Thus for e=1, 3∤N: d1=2, d2=p, d3=2p.

Now, we can compute f(N) = N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3.

Let's denote N = 2^e * m, m odd.

We'll compute f(N) in each case.

Case A1: 3|N, e≥2. Then d1=2,d2=3,d3=4. So f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = (12N + 8N + 6N)/24? Actually N/2 = 12N/24, N/3=8N/24, N/4=6N/24, sum=26N/24 = 13N/12. So f(N) = (13/12) N. Since N divisible by 12? Because e≥2 and 3|N, so N divisible by 12. So f(N) is integer.

Case A2: 3|N, e=1. Then d1=2,d2=3,d3 = 5 if 5|N, else 6. So:
- If 5|N: f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/5 = (15N + 10N + 6N)/30? Actually N/2=15N/30, N/3=10N/30, N/5=6N/30, sum=31N/30. So f(N) = (31/30) N. Since N=2*3*t, and 5|N implies 5|t, so N divisible by 30. So integer.
- If 5∤N: f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = (3N + 2N + N)/6 = 6N/6 = N. So f(N) = N. So N is a fixed point.

Thus for 3|N, e=1 and 5∤N, N is a fixed point. That's the class of fixed points we identified.

Case B: 3∤N, e≥2. Then d1=2,d2=4. Now d3 depends on e and the smallest odd prime factor p.

We need to consider subcases:

B1: e≥2 and 5|N. Then 5 is a divisor. Since 4<5, d3=5. Then f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/5 = (10N + 5N + 4N)/20 = 19N/20. So f(N) = (19/20) N. Since N divisible by 20? N=2^e*m with e≥2 and 5|N, so N divisible by 20 (since e≥2 gives factor 4, and 5 gives 5, so 20|N). So integer.

B2: e≥2, 5∤N, and e≥3. Then 8 is a divisor. Also the next smallest divisor could be 8 or some odd prime p≥7. Since 5∤N, the smallest odd prime factor p is at least 7. Compare 8 and p: if p=7, then 7<8, so d3=7 if 7|N. If p=11, then 8<11, so d3=8. If p=13, 8<13, d3=8. So we need to consider whether N is divisible by 7 or not. So:

B2a: e≥3 and 7|N. Then d3=7 (since 7<8). Then f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/7 = (14N + 7N + 4N)/28 = 25N/28. So f(N) = (25/28) N. Since N divisible by 28? N has factor 4 and 7, so 28|N. So integer.

B2b: e≥3 and 7∤N. Then d3=8 (since 8 divides N and 8 < next odd prime). Then f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/8 = (4N + 2N + N)/8 = 7N/8. So f(N) = (7/8) N. Since N divisible by 8, integer.

B3: e=2, 5∤N. Then N=4*m, m odd, and 5∤N. Also e=2 so 8 does not divide N. So d3: candidates: the next smallest divisor after 2 and 4. Since 5∤N, the smallest odd prime factor p≥7. Also 8 not divisor. So d3 = p (since p≥7, and no 4? Actually 4 is already d2, so after 4, the next divisor is either the smallest odd prime factor p or maybe a composite like 4*? But 4*2=8 not divisor, 4*3=12? But 3 not divisor? 3 may not divide N, but if 3 divides N, then we are in case A. So here 3∤N. So the divisors: 2,4, then the next smallest is either p (if p<4*? Actually 4*odd? But 4*odd=2*2*odd, that would be even and at least 4*5=20, but p could be 7, so p=7<20, so d3=p). Also 6? 6=2*3, but 3∤N, so not. So d3 = p (smallest odd prime factor). Then f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/p = (2N + N + 4N/p)/4? Actually N/2=2N/4, N/4=N/4, so sum = (3N/4) + N/p = (3N/4) + (N/p). Since N=4*m, N/p = 4m/p, which must be integer because p|m. So f(N) = 3m + 4m/p = m(3+4/p). Since p≥7, 4/p ≤ 4/7 ≈0.57, so f(N) is integer.

Thus for e=2, 3∤N, 5∤N: f(N) = N/2+N/4+N/p = (3/4)N + N/p.

We can compute f(N) = (3N/4) + (N/p) = (3N/4) + (N/p). Since N=4m, f(N)=3m+4m/p = m(3+4/p). So f(N) = m * (3p+4)/p.

Now, note that f(N) may be even or odd.

Case C: 3∤N, e=1. Then d1=2, d2=p (smallest odd prime factor), d3=2p. So f(N) = N/2 + N/p + N/(2p) = (2N/4?) Actually N=2*m, so N/2=m, N/p=2m/p, N/(2p)=m/p, so f(N)=m + 2m/p + m/p = m + 3m/p = m(1+3/p). So f(N) = m * (p+3)/p. Since p|m, f(N) integer.

Thus we have a full description of f(N) in terms of e and the odd part m.

Now, we need to find all N such that iterating f stays within numbers with at least three proper divisors. This is a functional graph. Possibly we can prove that the only possible starting numbers are those that eventually reach a fixed point, and the fixed points are exactly those with v2=1 and no factor 5, and the preimages of these fixed points are numbers that map to them. But we saw that numbers with v2=3 and no factor 5 can map to fixed points, and also numbers with v2=5 and no factor 5 can map to fixed points (e.g., 864->936->1014). So the set is all numbers that are in the "basin" of the fixed point set. This is reminiscent of problems where the transformation is "multiply by some rational factor" and then the sequence either decreases or increases but eventually stabilizes. We can try to analyze the growth factor.

From the formulas, we have:

- For 3|N, e≥2: f(N) = (13/12)N > N. So the sequence increases.
- For 3|N, e=1 and 5∤N: f(N)=N fixed.
- For 3|N, e=1 and 5|N: f(N)= (31/30)N > N. So increases.
- For 3∤N, e≥2 and 5|N: f(N)= (19/20)N < N. So decreases.
- For 3∤N, e≥2 and 5∤N, e≥3 and 7|N: f(N)= (25/28)N < N. So decreases.
- For 3∤N, e≥2 and 5∤N, e≥3 and 7∤N: f(N)= (7/8)N < N. So decreases.
- For 3∤N, e=2 and 5∤N: f(N)= m(3+4/p) = (3N/4) + N/p. Since N=4m, f(N)=3m+4m/p. Compare to N=4m: f(N) < N if 3+4/p < 4 => 4/p < 1 => p>4, which is true for p≥5. So f(N) < N. So decreases.
- For 3∤N, e=1: f(N)= m(1+3/p). Since m=N/2, f(N)=N/2 + 3N/(2p) = N(1/2+3/(2p)). For p≥5, 1/2+3/(2p) ≤ 1/2+3/10=0.8, so f(N) < N. So decreases.

Thus the only cases where f(N) > N are: (i) 3|N and e≥2: f(N) = (13/12)N > N; (ii) 3|N and e=1 and 5|N: f(N) = (31/30)N > N. So the sequence increases only in those cases. In all other cases, f(N) < N (or equal). So if we ever hit a number that is not in those increasing cases, the sequence will start decreasing. Since the sequence is positive integers, decreasing will eventually either hit a fixed point (if it becomes a fixed point) or hit a number with <3 proper divisors. But note that fixed points are also in the decreasing cases? Actually fixed points are with v2=1 and no factor 5. For these, f(N)=N, so they are constant. But if we start with a number that is not fixed, the sequence may increase for a while, but then eventually decrease to a fixed point or become prime. So we need to find all numbers that eventually lead to a fixed point.

Observe that the increasing cases only happen when N is divisible by 3 and either e≥2 or e=1 and 5|N. In those cases, f(N) is a rational multiple of N. So if we start with such N, the next number is larger. But then what about the next number? For f(N) to be in the increasing cases again, it must also be divisible by 3 and satisfy the conditions. So we need to study the orbit under the mapping.

Let's consider the transformation for numbers that are multiples of 3. Write N=3^a * t, where t not divisible by 3. Since N even, we have factor 2^e. So N = 2^e * 3^a * u, where u is coprime to 6. The condition for increasing cases: if e≥2, then f(N) = (13/12)N, which is (13/12)N. Since N is divisible by 12, f(N) is divisible by 13? Actually (13/12)N = 13*(N/12). So f(N) = 13 * (N/12). So f(N) is divisible by 13. Also note that f(N) is even? N/12 is integer, and 13 times that. Since N/12 is integer, and N is even, N/12 could be even or odd? For N=12, N/12=1, f=13. So f(N) is odd. For N=24, N/12=2, f=26 even. So f(N) may be even or odd.

Now, if f(N) is in the increasing cases again, we need it to be divisible by 3. Is f(N) divisible by 3? f(N)=13*(N/12). Since N is divisible by 3, N/12 is divisible by? If N=3^a*... with a≥1, then N/12 = 3^{a-1} * (2^{e-2} * u) because 12=2^2*3. So N/12 = 3^{a-1} * (2^{e-2} * u). So f(N) = 13 * 3^{a-1} * (2^{e-2} * u). So f(N) is divisible by 3 if a≥2. So if a=1, then f(N) is not divisible by 3. So if N has exactly one factor 3 (i.e., a=1), then f(N) is not divisible by 3. So after one step, the number loses the factor 3. Then it will be in the decreasing cases (since it's not divisible by 3). So the orbit will then decrease. Thus numbers with v3(N)=1 (exactly one factor 3) and e≥2 will increase once to a number not divisible by 3, then decrease. So they will eventually reach a fixed point if the decrease leads to a fixed point.

What about numbers with a≥2? Then f(N) is still divisible by 3. So the sequence may continue to increase as long as the exponent of 2 is at least 2 and the exponent of 3 is at least 2? Let's analyze: For N divisible by 3 and e≥2, f(N)=13*(N/12). So v2(f(N)) = v2(13*(N/12)) = v2(N/12) because 13 odd. So v2(f(N)) = v2(N) - 2 (since N/12 = 2^{v2(N)-2} * m). So if v2(N)=e, then v2(f(N)) = e-2. Also v3(f(N)) = v3(N) - 1. So the exponent of 3 decreases by 1, and the exponent of 2 decreases by 2. So if we start with N such that v2(N)≥2 and v3(N)≥1, then f(N) has v2' = e-2, v3' = a-1. So after one step, the exponent of 2 decreases by 2, and the exponent of 3 decreases by 1. So eventually, after enough steps, either v2 becomes 0 or v3 becomes 0. But note that the mapping f(N) = (13/12)N only applies when N is divisible by 12 and 3|N (which is automatic if N divisible by 12 and 3|N? Actually for f(N) = (13/12)N, we need e≥2 and 3|N. That's true if N divisible by 12. So if N is divisible by 12, then f(N) = 13*(N/12). So after one step, the new N' = 13*(N/12). Now, is N' divisible by 12? Not necessarily. It is divisible by 13, and maybe by 2 or 3? Since N divisible by 12, N/12 is integer. So N' = 13 * (N/12). So v2(N') = v2(N/12) = v2(N)-2, v3(N') = v3(N/12) = v3(N)-1. So for N' to be divisible by 12, we need v2(N')≥2 and v3(N')≥1. That is, v2(N)≥4 and v3(N)≥2. So if N has v2≥4 and v3≥2, then N' is divisible by 12. So the process can continue: each step reduces v2 by 2 and v3 by 1. So after k steps, we have v2' = v2(N)-2k, v3' = v3(N)-k. The process will continue as long as v2'≥2 and v3'≥1. So the number of steps we can have increasing behavior is limited. Eventually, either v2 becomes 0 or v3 becomes 0. At that point, the number will be either a power of 2 (if v3=0) or a number not divisible by 3 (if v3=0) or a number with v2<2. Then it will go into decreasing cases.

Thus the orbit will eventually become a number that is not divisible by 3 (or not divisible by 12) and then decrease. So the sequence will eventually become decreasing. Since it's decreasing, it will eventually hit a fixed point or become prime. But we need to ensure that it never hits a prime or prime square before hitting a fixed point. So we need to study the decreasing behavior.

From the formulas, when N is not divisible by 3, the mapping reduces N. The sequence will strictly decrease until it reaches a number that is either a fixed point or has <3 proper divisors. So we need to understand which numbers, when repeatedly applying f, will eventually land on a fixed point. Since the process is deterministic, we can try to characterize the set of numbers that are "good".

Observation: The decreasing cases are: N not divisible by 3, or N divisible by 3 but with e=1 and 5|N? Actually e=1 and 5|N gives f(N)=(31/30)N > N, so that's increasing. Thus if we start with a number that is not in those increasing cases, the sequence will be nonincreasing. If it ever hits a fixed point, it's good. If it hits a prime or prime square, it's bad. So we need to find all numbers that, when repeatedly applying f, eventually reach a fixed point.

Let's not get too deep.

Given the complexity, perhaps there is a known classification: The only possible a1 are those numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5, and also have the property that the exponent of 2 is not 2? But we saw 72 works, 864 works, 1008 fails, 1152 fails, 1224 works, etc. Let's try to find a pattern by computing v2 and v3 for good numbers.

Good numbers we have: 6 (v2=1,v3=1), 18 (v2=1,v3=2), 42 (v2=1,v3=1), 54 (v2=1,v3=3), 66 (v2=1,v3=1), 78 (v2=1,v3=1), 102 (v2=1,v3=1), 114 (v2=1,v3=1), 126 (v2=1,v3=2), 138 (v2=1,v3=1), 162 (v2=1,v3=4), 174 (v2=1,v3=1), 186 (v2=1,v3=1), 198 (v2=1,v3=1), 222 (v2=1,v3=1), 246 (v2=1,v3=1), 282 (v2=1,v3=1), 294 (v2=1,v3=1), 306 (v2=1,v3=2), 342 (v2=1,v3=2), 366 (v2=1,v3=1), 378 (v2=1,v3=3), 402 (v2=1,v3=1), 414 (v2=1,v3=1), 426 (v2=1,v3=1), 438 (v2=1,v3=1), 474 (v2=1,v3=1), 486 (v2=1,v3=4), 498 (v2=1,v3=1), 522 (v2=1,v3=1), etc. Also non-fixed good numbers: 72 (v2=3,v3=2), 216 (v2=3,v3=3), 504 (v2=3,v3=2), 648 (v2=3,v3=4), 792 (v2=3,v3=2), 936 (v2=3,v3=2), 1224 (v2=3,v3=1? Actually 1224=2^3*3^2*17, v3=2), 864 (v2=5,v3=3), etc. Also maybe 144? No. 288? No. 360? No. 384? No. 448? No. 480? No. 512? No. 576? No. 768? No. 1008? No. So the pattern seems to be that good numbers are those where v2 is not 2, and v3 is at least 1? But 36 has v2=2, v3=2, fails. 108 has v2=2, v3=3 fails. 132 has v2=2, v3=1 fails. So v2=2 always fails. What about v2=4? 16 fails (v3=0), 48 fails (v3=1), 80 fails (v3=0), 112 fails (v3=0), 144 fails (v3=2), 176 fails (v3=0), 192 fails (v3=1), 208 fails (v3=0), 224 fails (v3=0), 240 fails (v3=1), 256 fails (v3=0), 272 fails (v3=0), 288 fails (v3=2), 304 fails (v3=0), 320 fails (v3=0), 336 fails (v3=1), 352 fails (v3=0), 368 fails (v3=0), 384 fails (v3=1), 400 fails (v3=0), 416 fails (v3=0), 432 fails (v3=2), 448 fails (v3=0), 464 fails (v3=0), 480 fails (v3=1), 496 fails (v3=0), 512 fails (v3=0). So v2=4 fails. v2=6? 64 fails (v3=0), 96 fails (v3=1), 128 fails (v3=0), 160 fails (v3=0), 192 fails (v3=1), 224 fails (v3=0), 256 fails (v3=0), 288 fails (v3=2), 320 fails (v3=0), 352 fails (v3=0), 384 fails (v3=1), 416 fails (v3=0), 448 fails (v3=0), 480 fails (v3=1), 512 fails (v3=0), 576 fails (v3=2). So v2=6 fails. v2=7? 128 fails, 192 fails, 256 fails, 320 fails, 384 fails, 448 fails, 512 fails, 576 fails, 640 fails, 704 fails, 768 fails, 832 fails, 896 fails, 960 fails, 1024 fails? So v2=7 fails. v2=8? 256 fails, 384 fails, 512 fails, 640 fails, 768 fails, 896 fails, 1024 fails. So v2=8 fails. v2=9? 512 fails, 640 fails, 768 fails, 896 fails, 1024 fails. So v2=9 fails. v2=10? 1024 fails. So the only v2 that seem to work are 1,3,5? But we have 864 works (v2=5). 72 works (v2=3). 216 works (v2=3). 504 works (v2=3). 648 works (v2=3). 792 works (v2=3). 936 works (v2=3). 1224 works (v2=3). 864 works (v2=5). Are there any v2=7 that work? 128 fails, 192 fails, etc. So maybe v2 must be odd? But 1 works, 3 works, 5 works. 7 fails? Possibly all v2=7 fail. But we haven't tested 2^7*3^2=128*9=1152, we tested fails. 2^7*3=384, fails. 2^7*5=640, fails. 2^7*7=896, fails. So likely v2=7 fails. v2=9 fails. So maybe only v2=1,3,5? Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof. The proof will involve analyzing the transformation T, showing that if a1 does not satisfy the condition, then the sequence eventually hits a prime or prime square. And if it does satisfy, then the sequence is well-defined and infinite. So we need to prove both directions.

Given the complexity, we can structure the proof as follows:

1. Define f(N)=sum of three largest proper divisors of N.
2. Show that for N composite with at least three proper divisors, f(N) is an integer. Also note that if N is a prime or prime square, then f(N) is undefined (or the sequence stops). So we need to avoid those.

3. Characterize the fixed points: Numbers N such that f(N)=N. Show that these are exactly numbers divisible by 6, not divisible by 4, and not divisible by 5. (Proof: f(N)=N implies 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3=1, with d1,d2,d3 the three smallest divisors. The only solution with distinct d_i is (2,3,6). So N must be divisible by 2 and 3, not by 4, and not by 5 (since if 5|N, then 5 is a divisor, and the third smallest divisor would be 5, not 6, breaking the condition). Also N must be divisible by 6, and not divisible by 4, so v2(N)=1. And 5∤N. So fixed points are numbers of the form N=2*3*t with t odd and 5∤t.)

4. Now, consider the transformation for numbers that are not fixed points. Derive formulas for f(N) based on the 2-adic valuation and divisibility by 3. Use the three smallest divisors.

We can then analyze the orbit:

- If N is not divisible by 3, then f(N)<N. (Proof: d1=2, d2=p (smallest odd prime factor) or 4, and d3=2p or something; show that f(N)=N/2+N/p+N/(2p) < N/2+N/5+N/10? Actually we can show f(N) ≤ (3/4)N? Need to show strict inequality. For N even and not divisible by 3, the three smallest divisors are 2, p, 2p (where p≥5), so f(N)=N/2+N/p+N/(2p). Since p≥5, N/p ≤ N/5, N/(2p) ≤ N/10, so f(N) ≤ N/2+N/5+N/10 = 0.8N, so f(N)<N. So the sequence is decreasing.

- If N is divisible by 3 but v2(N) is even (i.e., not divisible by 4), then f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = N, so N is a fixed point. (Case A2: 3|N, e=1? Actually v2(N) even means N divisible by 2 but not by 4? That is v2(N)=1, which is odd. So even v2 means v2(N)≥2 and even, so N divisible by 4. Then f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/8? Wait we need to compute: For 3|N and e≥2, d1=2,d2=3,d3=4, so f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4 = (13/12)N > N. So if v2(N) even and ≥2, then f(N)>N. So the sequence increases. So that case leads to increasing behavior.

- If N is divisible by 3 and v2(N) is odd (≥3), then f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4 = (13/12)N > N, but also v2(N) reduces by 2, so after one step, v2' = v2(N)-2, which is odd again? Given the time, we can produce a solution that states the answer and gives a rigorous proof by analyzing the three smallest divisors and showing that the only possible a1 are those numbers that are divisible by 2^{2k+1} * 3^{k+1} for some k≥0, and not divisible by 5. We can also note that if a1 is divisible by 5, then it's impossible because the sequence would eventually become undefined. Thus the answer is: All positive integers N such that N is divisible by 6, the 2-adic valuation of N is odd, and the exponent of 3 in N is at least (v2(N)+1)/2, and N is not divisible by 5. Or more simply: N is a multiple of 2^{2k+1} * 3^{k+1} for some integer k≥0, and N is not divisible by 5.

Now, we need to present the proof. We'll break it into lemmas.

Lemma 1: For a composite integer N with at least three proper divisors, let d1<d2<d3 be the three smallest divisors of N (including N if necessary). Then the three largest proper divisors of N are N/d1, N/d2, N/d3, and the sum is T(N)=N/d1+N/d2+N/d3. Moreover, if N is a prime or a prime square, then it has fewer than three proper divisors, so T(N) is not defined.

Proof: Standard.

Lemma 2: The only solution in positive integers to 1/a+1/b+1/c=1 with a<b<c is (a,b,c)=(2,3,6). (Proof: Suppose a=2, then 1/b+1/c=1/2, and the only integer solution with b>2 is (3,6). If a=3, then 1/b+1/c=2/3, and b≥3 gives b=3,c=3 but then a=b, not allowed. If a=1, then 1/b+1/c=0 impossible. So only (2,3,6).)

Lemma 3: The fixed points of T are exactly numbers N such that N is divisible by 6, not divisible by 4, and not divisible by 5. (Proof: T(N)=N implies 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3=1, so (d1,d2,d3)=(2,3,6). Hence N must be divisible by 2 and 3, so N is a multiple of 6. Since d3=6, we need that 4 does not divide N (otherwise 4 would be a divisor less than 6) and 5 does not divide N (otherwise 5 would be a divisor less than 6). So v2(N)=1, 3|N, and 5∤N.)

Now, we analyze the behavior of T.

Define v2(N)=e, v3(N)=f. We'll consider cases.

Case I: 3∤N. Then the smallest divisors are 2, p (smallest odd prime factor), and 2p (since p≥5). Then T(N)=N/2+N/p+N/(2p) = N(1/2+1/p+1/(2p)). Thus for any N not divisible by 3, T(N) < N.

Case II: 3|N. Then we have subcases based on v2(N).

Subcase IIa: v2(N)=1. Then N is divisible by 2 but not by 4. Then the smallest divisors are 2,3, and then the next smallest divisor is either 5 if 5|N, or 6 if 5∤N. So we have:

- If 5|N: then d3=5, so T(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5 = (31/30)N. Since N divisible by 30, T(N) integer. Also T(N) > N because 31/30>1. So T(N) > N.
- If 5∤N: then d3=6, so T(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = N, so N is a fixed point.

Thus for v2(N)=1 and 5∤N, N is a fixed point.

Subcase IIb: v2(N)≥2 and even. Then N is divisible by 4. The smallest divisors are 2,3,4 (since 4 divides N, and 3 divides N). So T(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4 = (13/12)N > N. So T(N) > N.

Subcase IIc: v2(N)≥3 and odd. Then N is divisible by 8. The smallest divisors are 2,3,4 (since 4 divides N). So T(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4 = (13/12)N > N. So T(N) > N.

Thus when 3|N and v2(N)≥2, we have T(N) > N.

We need to solve: "A proper divisor of a positive integer N is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. The infinite sequence a1, a2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n ≥ 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a1."

Interpretation: We have a sequence defined recursively: a_{n+1} = sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Each a_n must have at least three proper divisors, so the sum is defined. We need to find all initial values a1 such that the sequence is infinite (i.e., always defined, meaning each a_n has at least three proper divisors). Possibly the sequence might become undefined if at some point a_n has fewer than three proper divisors; we need to avoid that.

Thus we need to characterize positive integers N that, when we repeatedly take the sum of the three largest proper divisors, we never encounter a number with fewer than three proper divisors. In other words, the sequence must be well-defined for all n.

We need to determine all possible a1.

Now, are there any other fixed points where d1,d2,d3 are not (2,3,6) but still 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3=1? But careful: If N has at least three proper divisors, then the three largest proper divisors are the three largest divisors less than N. In terms of the divisors, if we list all divisors in increasing order: 1 = d0 < d1 < d2 < ... < d_{k-1} < d_k = N. Then proper divisors are d0,d1,...,d_{k-1}. The three largest proper divisors are d_{k-1}, d_{k-2}, d_{k-3}. And we have d_i * d_{k-i} = N. So d_{k-1} = N/d1, d_{k-2} = N/d2, d_{k-3} = N/d3. So indeed T(N)=N/d1+N/d2+N/d3. We have T(6)=6, T(18)=18, T(42)=42, T(54)=54, T(66)=66, T(78)=78, T(102)=102, T(114)=114, T(126)=126, T(138)=138, T(162)=162, T(174)=174, T(186)=186, T(198)=198, etc. So these are fixed points. That set might be all numbers that are "good". But we need to determine if there are any numbers that never reach a fixed point but still stay within the set (e.g., cycles). Thus we need a systematic characterization.

Given the recurrence, we can try to analyze the behavior in terms of the prime factorization. Let's denote N. Write its prime factorization. We need to find all N such that the orbit never hits a number with <3 proper divisors.

Observation: If N is a fixed point (multiple of 6, not divisible by 4 or 5), then it works.

Now, what about numbers that map to a fixed point? For such numbers, the orbit will become constant after one step. So they are good if they themselves have at least three proper divisors. So we need to find all even N such that T(N) is a fixed point (i.e., multiple of 6, not divisible by 4 or 5). And also N itself must have at least three proper divisors. So the set of good numbers includes all numbers that eventually reach a fixed point. Could there be numbers that map to a fixed point after more than one step? That would be numbers that map to a good number that is not fixed, but eventually becomes fixed. So the set of good numbers is the preimage of the set of fixed points under T, and also the preimage of those preimages, etc. In other words, it is the set of numbers that are in the "basin of attraction" of the fixed points. So we need to find the entire set of numbers that eventually land on a fixed point.

Now, could there be numbers that never land on a fixed point but still stay in the set? Possibly cycles of length >1. But we haven't found any. Let's try to see if a cycle could exist. Suppose we have a cycle. Since T(N) = N/d1+N/d2+N/d3, and d1,d2,d3 are divisors, we have that T(N) is at least N/2+? Actually for even N, d1=2, so T(N)=N/2+N/d2+N/d3. Since d2,d3≥2, we have T(N) ≤ N/2+N/2+N/2 = 3N/2. But also, if N is not divisible by 2? Already even. So T(N) could be less than N if N/d2+N/d3 < N/2. For odd N, T(N) < N. So the sequence is strictly decreasing until it becomes even? Actually for even N, T(N) could be less than N, equal to N, or greater than N. We can try to analyze the recursion by considering the exponent of 2 and the odd part. Let's define N. Write N = 2^e * m, with m odd.

We need to compute d1,d2,d3, the three smallest divisors >1. Since 2 is a divisor, d1=2. Then d2: the next smallest divisor. If e≥2, then 4 is a divisor. So the second smallest divisor is min(3,4) if both divide N, else the smallest odd prime factor if N not divisible by 3, else 3. So we need to consider cases based on whether N is divisible by 3 and whether e≥2.

Case 1: N is not divisible by 3. Then the smallest odd prime factor p (p≥5). Since N is even, d2 = p if p ≤ 4? But p≥5, so d2 = p. And d3: the next smallest divisor. It could be 4 if e≥2, or p^2 if p^2 divides N, or maybe 2p? Actually the divisors in increasing order: 2, then p, then either 4 (if e≥2) or 2p? Since p is odd, 2p is even and may be less than p^2 if p>2? For p=5, 2p=10, p^2=25, so 10 < 25. But is 2p a divisor? Yes, because 2 divides N and p divides N, so 2p divides N. So after 2 and p, the next smallest divisor could be 4 if e≥2 and 4 < 2p? Compare 4 and 2p: for p≥5, 2p≥10, so 4 < 2p. So if e≥2, then 4 is a divisor and 4 < 2p. So d3 = 4. If e=1, then 4 is not a divisor, and the next divisor after 2 and p is 2p? Since p^2 > 2p for p>2? For p=5, p^2=25, 2p=10, so 2p < p^2. So d3 = 2p. But also there might be a composite divisor like p*q? But q > p, so 2p is smaller. So d3 = 2p. So we have two subcases: e≥2 and e=1.

Case 2: N is divisible by 3. Then d2=3. Then d3: the next smallest divisor. It could be 4 if e≥2, or 6? Actually after 2 and 3, the next smallest divisor could be 4 (if e≥2) or 6 (since 6 divides N). Compare 4 and 6: 4 < 6. So if e≥2, then d3=4. If e=1, then 4 is not a divisor, and the next smallest divisor is 6 (since 6=2*3 divides N). So d3=6. But also there might be other divisors like 8? But 8 would be larger than 6 if e≥3? Actually if e≥3, then 8 is a divisor, but compare 8 and 6: 6 < 8, so d3 is still 4 if e≥2? Wait we need the third smallest divisor >1. So the sequence of divisors in increasing order: 2,3,... Then the next candidate is 4 (if e≥2) because 4 < 6. So d3=4. For e=1, the next is 6 because 6 < other divisors like 9? But 9 might be divisor if 9|N, but 6 < 9. So d3=6.

Thus in all cases, we can determine d1,d2,d3 based on e and whether 3|N.

Now, T(N)=N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3.

We can then analyze the parity and divisibility by 5 and 3.

Our goal: Find all N such that the sequence never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. We have already seen that if N is a fixed point (i.e., T(N)=N), then it works. Largest three:333,111,37 sum=501->? 501=3*167, sum=171->? 171=9*19, sum=77->19 fails. So all odd multiples of 3 fail. So it seems that numbers that are not divisible by 6 (i.e., not divisible by 2 and 3) fail. Numbers divisible by 6 work only if they are fixed points (exponent of 2 = 1, odd part multiple of 3) or if they are of the form 72k with k odd and not divisible by 5? But wait, what about numbers divisible by 6 with exponent of 2 = 3? That's 24*3=72, 24*9=216, 24*15=360 (contains 5), 24*21=504, 24*27=648, 24*33=792, 24*39=936, 24*45=1080 (contains 5), etc. So 72k with k odd and not divisible by 5. But also note that 72*1=72 works, 72*3=216 works, 72*5=360 fails because 5 divides 360, 72*7=504 works, 72*9=648 works, 72*11=792? Let's test 792. 792=2^3*3^2*11? 72=2^3*3^2, 11 odd, so N=72*11=792, odd part=3^2*11=99, not divisible by 5, so should work. T(792)=? 792/2=396, 792/3=264, 792/4=198, sum=858. 858=2*3*11*13, divisible by 2? 858/2=429, not divisible by 4, divisible by 3? Yes, 858/6=143, so 858=2*3*11*13. Is 858 a fixed point? Check: 858 mod4=2, mod5=3, so fixed point. So 792 works. So indeed all numbers of the form 72k with k odd and 5∤k are good.

But what about numbers with exponent of 2 = 3 and odd part divisible by 3 but not necessarily by 9? For example, N=2^3*3=24, we saw fails. So the odd part must be divisible by 9. So N must be divisible by 72. So the good numbers that are not fixed points are exactly those numbers N that are multiples of 72 and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. But also we have fixed points: numbers N that are multiples of 6, not divisible by 4 or 5, and N/6 is odd? Wait fixed points: N=2 * (odd multiple of 3) with no factor 5. That is, N=6 * (odd) with odd divisible by 3? Actually if N=2*m, with m odd, then N divisible by 6 means 3|m, so m=3*t, t odd. So N=6t, t odd. And 5∤N means 5∤t. So fixed points are N=6t with t odd and 5∤t. But note that t must be odd, so N/6 is odd. So fixed points are multiples of 6 with N/6 odd and not divisible by 5. So these are also numbers of the form 6t where t is odd and not multiple of 5. That includes t=1 (6), t=3 (18), t=7 (42), t=9 (54), t=11 (66), t=13 (78), t=17 (102), t=19 (114), t=21 (126), t=23 (138), t=27 (162), t=29 (174), t=31 (186), t=33 (198), t=37 (222), t=39 (234), t=41 (246), t=43 (258)? 258=6*43, 43 odd not multiple of 5, so 258 should be fixed? Check: 258=2*3*43, so d1=2,d2=3,d3=6 => T=129+86+43=258, yes fixed. So 258 works. So fixed points are N=6t with t odd and 5∤t. So these are all numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5? Because if N=6t, with t odd, then N is divisible by 2 but not by 4 (since t odd, N=2*3t, and 3t odd, so only one factor 2). So indeed fixed points are numbers divisible by 6, not divisible by 4, and not divisible by 5. That's the condition.

Now, the good numbers we found are: fixed points (exponent of 2 = 1, odd part multiple of 3, no factor 5) and numbers of the form 72k with k odd and not divisible by 5. Now, are there numbers that map to numbers of the form 72k (which are not fixed points) but are not themselves of the form 72k? That is, numbers N such that T(N) = 72k, with k odd and 5∤k. Then N would also be good. So we need to find all N such that T(N) is in the set S = {72k : k odd, 5∤k}. This is the preimage of S. Then we can iterate. So the set of good numbers is the union of all preimages of the fixed point set under iterates of T. This is essentially the set of numbers that eventually reach a fixed point.

Given the complexity, maybe we can prove that the only numbers that are good are those that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5. But we have counterexample: 36 is multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, but fails. So not that.

Let's try to find a structural characterization. Perhaps we can consider the transformation in terms of the "odd part" and the "2-adic valuation". Let's denote v2(N)=e. Write N=2^e * m, with m odd. Then the three smallest divisors are: 2, then the smallest odd divisor >1, which is the smallest odd prime factor p (if m>1) or 3? Actually if N is divisible by 3, then p=3. It could be 4 if e≥2, or 2p if e=1 and p>3, or 6 if e≥2 and d2=3, or 2*3=6 if e=1 and d2=3, etc. So we need to analyze cases based on e and whether 3|N.

Our goal: Find all N such that the sequence never hits a prime or prime square. Since the sequence is defined by T, and if we ever hit a fixed point, we are done. So we need to find all N that eventually reach a fixed point. This is a typical problem where the transformation is "reducing" in some sense. Perhaps we can show that if N is not a fixed point, then T(N) is either less than N or eventually becomes less. But we saw T(N) > N for many numbers. However, maybe we can show that if N is not a fixed point, then the process eventually decreases, and the only numbers that can increase are those that are "good" and eventually become fixed. So perhaps we can characterize all numbers that are not in the "basin" of primes.

Thus the good numbers are those that eventually reach a fixed point. Now, we have numbers with v2=3 that work: 72,216,504,648,792,936, etc. But also 864 has v2=5 works. So the condition is not simply about v2. Let's analyze the transformation in terms of the exponent of 2 and the odd part. Perhaps we can show that if a1 is not a fixed point, then the sequence will eventually become a fixed point if and only if a1 is divisible by 6 and not by 5, and also a1 is not in the set of numbers that map to a prime or prime square. But we need to determine all such a1. This seems like a complex classification.

Maybe we can approach by considering the process as "replace N by N/d1+N/d2+N/d3". We can think of the transformation as f(N). For N even, we have f(N)=N/2+N/d2+N/d3. Since d2 and d3 are divisors, we can write N=2^e*m. Then we can compute f(N) in terms of e and m.

Let's do a systematic analysis of cases based on e and whether 3|N.

Let N=2^e*m, m odd, m>1 (if m=1 then N is a power of 2, which fails because it has only one proper divisor? Actually 2^e with e≥2 has proper divisors: 1,2,4,...,2^{e-1}. It has at least three proper divisors if e≥3? For e=3, N=8, proper divisors:1,2,4 -> three, but T(8)=7, which is prime. So powers of 2 fail. For e=4, N=16, proper divisors:1,2,4,8 -> T=8+4+2=14, 14->10->... fails. So all powers of 2 fail. So we can assume m>1.

Now, we need to consider the three smallest divisors.

The divisors in increasing order: 1, d1, d2, d3, ... where d1 is the smallest divisor >1, which is 2 (since N even). Then d2 is the next smallest divisor >1. It could be 3 if 3|N, else it could be 4 if 4|N, else it could be some odd prime p≥5. Since N is even and m>1, there is at least one odd divisor >1. The smallest odd divisor >1 is the smallest odd prime factor of N, call it p. So d2 = min(3, p, 4?) Actually if 3 divides N, then p=3, so d2=3. If 3 does not divide N, then d2 = p if p<4? But p≥5, so d2=p. But also 4 may be a divisor if e≥2. If e≥2, then 4 is a divisor. So when 3∤N, we have two candidates: p and 4. Since p≥5, 4 < p, so the second smallest divisor is 4 if e≥2. If e=1, then 4 is not a divisor, so d2 = p. So we have:

- If 3|N: d2 = 3.
- If 3∤N and e≥2: d2 = 4.
- If 3∤N and e=1: d2 = p (the smallest odd prime factor).

Now, d3: the next smallest divisor after d1 and d2. We need to consider cases.

Case A: 3|N. Then d1=2, d2=3. Now d3: the next smallest divisor could be 4 if e≥2, or 6 if e=1? But also if 5 divides N, then 5 is a divisor; compare 5 and 4? 4<5, so if e≥2, d3=4. If e=1, then 4 not divisor. Then the next smallest divisor after 2 and 3 is either 5 if 5|N, or 6 if 6|N? But 6=2*3, and since N even and 3|N, 6|N. So 6 is a divisor. Compare 5 and 6: 5<6, so if 5|N, then d3=5. If 5∤N, then d3=6. So:
- If e≥2: d3=4.
- If e=1: d3 = min(5,6) if both divide; if 5∤N then d3=6.

Thus for 3|N:
- e≥2: d1=2,d2=3,d3=4.
- e=1: d1=2,d2=3,d3 = 5 if 5|N, else 6.

Case B: 3∤N and e≥2. Then d1=2,d2=4. Now d3: the next smallest divisor after 2 and 4. Candidates: 8 if e≥3, or 3? But 3∤N, so 3 not divisor. The next could be 5 if 5|N, or 6? But 6=2*3, and 3∤N, so 6 not divisor. So after 2 and 4, the next smallest divisor is either 5 (if 5|N) or 8 (if e≥3) or maybe a prime p≥5. Since p≥5, and 5<8, so if 5|N, then d3=5. If 5∤N, then d3 = min(8, next odd prime p) but p≥7, and 8<7? No, 8>7. So if e≥3, then 8 is a divisor, and if there is an odd prime p=7, then 7<8, so d3=7 if 7|N. So d3 is the smallest divisor among: 5,7,8, etc. So we need to consider the smallest odd prime factor p. Since 3∤N, p≥5. So d3 = min(5,8, p) if both 5 and 8 are divisors? But 8 is only if e≥3. So we have subcases based on e and p.

Case C: 3∤N and e=1. Then d1=2,d2=p (smallest odd prime factor). Now d3: the next smallest divisor after 2 and p. Candidates: 2p (since 2 and p divide N, so 2p|N). Also 4? e=1 so 4 not divisor. Also p^2? If p^2 divides N, then p^2 may be larger than 2p for p≥5? 2p=10, p^2=25, so 2p < p^2. So d3 = 2p (since 2p is a divisor). But also if there is another odd prime q>p, then 2q? That would be larger than 2p? 2p=10, 2q≥14, so 2p is smaller. So d3 = 2p. However, if p=5 and also 3 divides N? But 3∤N. So d3=2p.

Thus for e=1, 3∤N: d1=2, d2=p, d3=2p.

Now, we can compute f(N) = N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3.

Let's denote N = 2^e * m, m odd.

We'll compute f(N) in each case.

Case A1: 3|N, e≥2. Then d1=2,d2=3,d3=4. So f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = (12N + 8N + 6N)/24? Actually N/2 = 12N/24, N/3=8N/24, N/4=6N/24, sum=26N/24 = 13N/12. So f(N) = (13/12) N. Since N divisible by 12? Because e≥2 and 3|N, so N divisible by 12. So f(N) is integer.

Case A2: 3|N, e=1. Then d1=2,d2=3,d3 = 5 if 5|N, else 6. So:
- If 5|N: f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/5 = (15N + 10N + 6N)/30? Actually N/2=15N/30, N/3=10N/30, N/5=6N/30, sum=31N/30. So f(N) = (31/30) N. Since N=2*3*t, and 5|N implies 5|t, so N divisible by 30. So integer.
- If 5∤N: f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = (3N + 2N + N)/6 = 6N/6 = N. So f(N) = N. So N is a fixed point.

Thus for 3|N, e=1 and 5∤N, N is a fixed point. That's the class of fixed points we identified.

Case B: 3∤N, e≥2. Then d1=2,d2=4. Now d3 depends on e and the smallest odd prime factor p.

We need to consider subcases:

B1: e≥2 and 5|N. Then 5 is a divisor. Since 4<5, d3=5. Then f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/5 = (10N + 5N + 4N)/20 = 19N/20. So f(N) = (19/20) N. Since N divisible by 20? N=2^e*m with e≥2 and 5|N, so N divisible by 20 (since e≥2 gives factor 4, and 5 gives 5, so 20|N). So integer.

B2: e≥2, 5∤N, and e≥3. Then 8 is a divisor. Also the next smallest divisor could be 8 or some odd prime p≥7. Since 5∤N, the smallest odd prime factor p is at least 7. Compare 8 and p: if p=7, then 7<8, so d3=7 if 7|N. If p=11, then 8<11, so d3=8. If p=13, 8<13, d3=8. So we need to consider whether N is divisible by 7 or not. So:

B2a: e≥3 and 7|N. Then d3=7 (since 7<8). Then f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/7 = (14N + 7N + 4N)/28 = 25N/28. So f(N) = (25/28) N. Since N divisible by 28? N has factor 4 and 7, so 28|N. So integer.

B2b: e≥3 and 7∤N. Then d3=8 (since 8 divides N and 8 < next odd prime). Then f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/8 = (4N + 2N + N)/8 = 7N/8. So f(N) = (7/8) N. Since N divisible by 8, integer.

B3: e=2, 5∤N. Then N=4*m, m odd, and 5∤N. Also e=2 so 8 does not divide N. So d3: candidates: the next smallest divisor after 2 and 4. Since 5∤N, the smallest odd prime factor p≥7. Also 8 not divisor. So d3 = p (since p≥7, and no 4? Actually 4 is already d2, so after 4, the next divisor is either the smallest odd prime factor p or maybe a composite like 4*? But 4*2=8 not divisor, 4*3=12? But 3 not divisor? 3 may not divide N, but if 3 divides N, then we are in case A. So here 3∤N. So the divisors: 2,4, then the next smallest is either p (if p<4*? Actually 4*odd? But 4*odd=2*2*odd, that would be even and at least 4*5=20, but p could be 7, so p=7<20, so d3=p). Also 6? 6=2*3, but 3∤N, so not. So d3 = p (smallest odd prime factor). Then f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/p = (2N + N + 4N/p)/4? Actually N/2=2N/4, N/4=N/4, so sum = (3N/4) + N/p = (3N/4) + (N/p). Since N=4*m, N/p = 4m/p, which must be integer because p|m. So f(N) = 3m + 4m/p = m(3+4/p). Since p≥7, 4/p ≤ 4/7 ≈0.57, so f(N) is integer.

Thus for e=2, 3∤N, 5∤N: f(N) = N/2+N/4+N/p = (3/4)N + N/p.

We can compute f(N) = (3N/4) + (N/p) = (3N/4) + (N/p). Since N=4m, f(N)=3m+4m/p = m(3+4/p). So f(N) = m * (3p+4)/p.

Now, note that f(N) may be even or odd.

Case C: 3∤N, e=1. Then d1=2, d2=p (smallest odd prime factor), d3=2p. So f(N) = N/2 + N/p + N/(2p) = (2N/4?) Actually N=2*m, so N/2=m, N/p=2m/p, N/(2p)=m/p, so f(N)=m + 2m/p + m/p = m + 3m/p = m(1+3/p). So f(N) = m * (p+3)/p. Since p|m, f(N) integer.

Thus we have a full description of f(N) in terms of e and the odd part m.

Now, we need to find all N such that iterating f stays within numbers with at least three proper divisors. This is a functional graph. Possibly we can prove that the only possible starting numbers are those that eventually reach a fixed point, and the fixed points are exactly those with v2=1 and no factor 5, and the preimages of these fixed points are numbers that map to them. But we saw that numbers with v2=3 and no factor 5 can map to fixed points, and also numbers with v2=5 and no factor 5 can map to fixed points (e.g., 864->936->1014). So the set is all numbers that are in the "basin" of the fixed point set. This is reminiscent of problems where the transformation is "multiply by some rational factor" and then the sequence either decreases or increases but eventually stabilizes. We can try to analyze the growth factor.

From the formulas, we have:

- For 3|N, e≥2: f(N) = (13/12)N > N. So the sequence increases.
- For 3|N, e=1 and 5∤N: f(N)=N fixed.
- For 3|N, e=1 and 5|N: f(N)= (31/30)N > N. So increases.
- For 3∤N, e≥2 and 5|N: f(N)= (19/20)N < N. So decreases.
- For 3∤N, e≥2 and 5∤N, e≥3 and 7|N: f(N)= (25/28)N < N. So decreases.
- For 3∤N, e≥2 and 5∤N, e≥3 and 7∤N: f(N)= (7/8)N < N. So decreases.
- For 3∤N, e=2 and 5∤N: f(N)= m(3+4/p) = (3N/4) + N/p. Since N=4m, f(N)=3m+4m/p. Compare to N=4m: f(N) < N if 3+4/p < 4 => 4/p < 1 => p>4, which is true for p≥5. So f(N) < N. So decreases.
- For 3∤N, e=1: f(N)= m(1+3/p). Since m=N/2, f(N)=N/2 + 3N/(2p) = N(1/2+3/(2p)). For p≥5, 1/2+3/(2p) ≤ 1/2+3/10=0.8, so f(N) < N. So decreases.

Thus the only cases where f(N) > N are: (i) 3|N and e≥2: f(N) = (13/12)N > N; (ii) 3|N and e=1 and 5|N: f(N) = (31/30)N > N. So the sequence increases only in those cases. In all other cases, f(N) < N (or equal). So if we ever hit a number that is not in those increasing cases, the sequence will start decreasing. Since the sequence is positive integers, decreasing will eventually either hit a fixed point (if it becomes a fixed point) or hit a number with <3 proper divisors. But note that fixed points are also in the decreasing cases? Actually fixed points are with v2=1 and no factor 5. For these, f(N)=N, so they are constant. But if we start with a number that is not fixed, the sequence may increase for a while, but then eventually decrease to a fixed point or become prime. So we need to find all numbers that eventually lead to a fixed point.

Observe that the increasing cases only happen when N is divisible by 3 and either e≥2 or e=1 and 5|N. In those cases, f(N) is a rational multiple of N. So if we start with such N, the next number is larger. But then what about the next number? For f(N) to be in the increasing cases again, it must also be divisible by 3 and satisfy the conditions. So we need to study the orbit under the mapping.

Let's consider the transformation for numbers that are multiples of 3. Write N=3^a * t, where t not divisible by 3. Since N even, we have factor 2^e. So N = 2^e * 3^a * u, where u is coprime to 6. The condition for increasing cases: if e≥2, then f(N) = (13/12)N, which is (13/12)N. Since N is divisible by 12, f(N) is divisible by 13? Actually (13/12)N = 13*(N/12). So f(N) = 13 * (N/12). So f(N) is divisible by 13. Also note that f(N) is even? N/12 is integer, and 13 times that. Since N/12 is integer, and N is even, N/12 could be even or odd? For N=12, N/12=1, f=13. So f(N) is odd. For N=24, N/12=2, f=26 even. So f(N) may be even or odd.

Now, if f(N) is in the increasing cases again, we need it to be divisible by 3. Is f(N) divisible by 3? f(N)=13*(N/12). Since N is divisible by 3, N/12 is divisible by? If N=3^a*... with a≥1, then N/12 = 3^{a-1} * (2^{e-2} * u) because 12=2^2*3. So N/12 = 3^{a-1} * (2^{e-2} * u). So f(N) = 13 * 3^{a-1} * (2^{e-2} * u). So f(N) is divisible by 3 if a≥2. So if a=1, then f(N) is not divisible by 3. So if N has exactly one factor 3 (i.e., a=1), then f(N) is not divisible by 3. So after one step, the number loses the factor 3. Then it will be in the decreasing cases (since it's not divisible by 3). So the orbit will then decrease. Thus numbers with v3(N)=1 (exactly one factor 3) and e≥2 will increase once to a number not divisible by 3, then decrease. So they will eventually reach a fixed point if the decrease leads to a fixed point.

What about numbers with a≥2? Then f(N) is still divisible by 3. So the sequence may continue to increase as long as the exponent of 2 is at least 2 and the exponent of 3 is at least 2? Let's analyze: For N divisible by 3 and e≥2, f(N)=13*(N/12). So v2(f(N)) = v2(13*(N/12)) = v2(N/12) because 13 odd. So v2(f(N)) = v2(N) - 2 (since N/12 = 2^{v2(N)-2} * m). So if v2(N)=e, then v2(f(N)) = e-2. Also v3(f(N)) = v3(N) - 1. So the exponent of 3 decreases by 1, and the exponent of 2 decreases by 2. So if we start with N such that v2(N)≥2 and v3(N)≥1, then f(N) has v2' = e-2, v3' = a-1. So after one step, the exponent of 2 decreases by 2, and the exponent of 3 decreases by 1. So eventually, after enough steps, either v2 becomes 0 or v3 becomes 0. But note that the mapping f(N) = (13/12)N only applies when N is divisible by 12 and 3|N (which is automatic if N divisible by 12 and 3|N? Actually for f(N) = (13/12)N, we need e≥2 and 3|N. That's true if N divisible by 12. So if N is divisible by 12, then f(N) = 13*(N/12). So after one step, the new N' = 13*(N/12). Now, is N' divisible by 12? Not necessarily. It is divisible by 13, and maybe by 2 or 3? Since N divisible by 12, N/12 is integer. So N' = 13 * (N/12). So v2(N') = v2(N/12) = v2(N)-2, v3(N') = v3(N/12) = v3(N)-1. So for N' to be divisible by 12, we need v2(N')≥2 and v3(N')≥1. That is, v2(N)≥4 and v3(N)≥2. So if N has v2≥4 and v3≥2, then N' is divisible by 12. So the process can continue: each step reduces v2 by 2 and v3 by 1. So after k steps, we have v2' = v2(N)-2k, v3' = v3(N)-k. The process will continue as long as v2'≥2 and v3'≥1. So the number of steps we can have increasing behavior is limited. Eventually, either v2 becomes 0 or v3 becomes 0. At that point, the number will be either a power of 2 (if v3=0) or a number not divisible by 3 (if v3=0) or a number with v2<2. Then it will go into decreasing cases.

Thus the orbit will eventually become a number that is not divisible by 3 (or not divisible by 12) and then decrease. So the sequence will eventually become decreasing. Since it's decreasing, it will eventually hit a fixed point or become prime. But we need to ensure that it never hits a prime or prime square before hitting a fixed point. So we need to study the decreasing behavior.

From the formulas, when N is not divisible by 3, the mapping reduces N. The sequence will strictly decrease until it reaches a number that is either a fixed point or has <3 proper divisors. So we need to understand which numbers, when repeatedly applying f, will eventually land on a fixed point. Since the process is deterministic, we can try to characterize the set of numbers that are "good".

Observation: The decreasing cases are: N not divisible by 3, or N divisible by 3 but with e=1 and 5|N? Actually e=1 and 5|N gives f(N)=(31/30)N > N, so that's increasing. So for N not divisible by 3, we have f(N) < N. Also for N divisible by 3 with e=1 and 5∤N, f(N)=N fixed, so that's constant. For N divisible by 3 with e=1 and 5|N, f(N)>N, so that's increasing. So the only cases where f(N) > N are: N divisible by 3 and (e≥2 or e=1 and 5|N). In all other cases, f(N) ≤ N.

Thus if we start with a number that is not in those increasing cases, the sequence will be nonincreasing. If it ever hits a fixed point, it's good. If it hits a prime or prime square, it's bad. So we need to find all numbers that, when repeatedly applying f, eventually reach a fixed point.

Given that the mapping for numbers not divisible by 3 is f(N) = something that is less than N. So we can think of the "reduction" process. We can try to find a characterization of numbers that are in the "basin" of the fixed points. Since the fixed points are numbers with v2=1 and no factor 5 and divisible by 3. So any number that eventually reduces to such a number is good.

Perhaps we can prove that if a1 is divisible by 6 and not divisible by 5, then the sequence is infinite. But we have counterexample 36. 36 is divisible by 6, not divisible by 5, but fails. So maybe the condition is that v2(a1) ≠ 2? That is, if v2=2, then it fails. 36 has v2=2. 108 has v2=2 fails. 132 v2=2 fails. 156 v2=2 fails. So v2=2 seems always failing. What about v2=4? 16 fails, 48 fails, 80 fails, 112 fails, 144 fails, etc. So v2=4 fails. v2=6? 64 fails, 192 fails, etc. So v2≥2 fails? But we have 72 has v2=3 works, 216 v2=3 works, 864 v2=5 works. So v2=3 works, v2=5 works? 864 works. So v2=3 and 5 work. So v2=2 fails, v2=4 fails, v2=6? Now, also note that if v2 is odd but v3 is less than (v2+1)/2, then the number is not divisible by that specific product, and we saw that 24 (v2=3,v3=1) fails; 48 (v2=4? Thus the set of possible a1 is: All positive integers N such that N is divisible by 2^{2k+1} * 3^{k+1} for some integer k≥0 (i.e., v2(N) is odd and v3(N) ≥ (v2(N)+1)/2), and 5 does not divide N. Also note that if N is a power of 2, v3=0, so fails. If N is a multiple of 6 but v2 even, fails (like 12,24? 24 fails because v3=1 not enough, and v2=3 is odd but v3=1 not enough; 24 fails). So the condition seems to be: N is a multiple of 6 and the 2-adic valuation is odd, and the exponent of 3 is at least (v2+1)/2. Also N not divisible by 5.

But is that necessary? We have seen that numbers with v2=1 and v3≥1 work as long as no 5. For v2=1, (v2+1)/2=1, so v3≥1 is required. So that's consistent. For v2=3, (v2+1)/2=2, so v3≥2 required. For v2=5, (v2+1)/2=3, so v3≥3 required. So the condition is exactly: v2(N) is odd and v3(N) ≥ (v2(N)+1)/2. Also 5∤N.

But is it possible that a number with v2 even could work? For example, 36 has v2=2 even, fails. 48 v2=4 even fails. 60 v2=2 fails. 84 v2=2 fails. 120 v2=3? 120=2^3*3*5, v2=3 odd, v3=1, (v2+1)/2=2, v3=1<2, fails. So no even v2 works. What about v2=0? That's odd numbers, all fail. So only odd v2 numbers with sufficient exponent of 3 work.

Now, we should also consider the possibility that the sequence might eventually become periodic with period >1 but still avoid primes. We haven't found any such cycles. Could there be a cycle like N -> M -> N with both N and M good? For that, we would need N and M to be in the basin of fixed points? But fixed points are constant. If there is a 2-cycle, then both numbers would have to map to each other, and both would have to have at least three proper divisors. Could such a cycle exist? Let's try to see if any number maps to a number that is not fixed, but then maps back. For instance, if we have N such that f(N)=M and f(M)=N, then N and M must have the same properties? Since f is not injective, it's possible. But given our derived formulas, we can try to see if any number with v2 odd and v3≥(v2+1)/2 can map to another such number? For numbers with v2≥2, we have f(N)=13*(N/12) which reduces v2 by 2 and v3 by 1. So the orbit under the "increasing" step reduces the exponents. So if we start with N with v2 odd and v3≥(v2+1)/2, then after applying f repeatedly, we get numbers with decreasing v2 and v3, eventually reaching a number with v2=1 and v3≥1, which is a fixed point. So no cycles.

Thus the only good numbers are those that eventually land on a fixed point. And we have characterized them as numbers N such that N is divisible by 2^{2k+1} * 3^{k+1} for some k≥0, and 5 ∤ N. But we also need that N itself has at least three proper divisors. This condition is automatically satisfied if N is divisible by 2 and 3 and not a prime square? For N divisible by 6, it has at least divisors 1,2,3,6, so at least three proper divisors (unless N=6? 6 has proper divisors 1,2,3 -> three). So that's fine.

Now, we must also consider the possibility that a1 is such that after some steps, we get a number that is divisible by 5, which then might cause the sequence to become undefined? But if a1 is not divisible by 5, the orbit may eventually become divisible by 5? For example, 72 is divisible by 2^3*3^2, no 5, but T(72)=78, no 5. So 5 doesn't appear. 216->234, no 5. 504->546, no 5. So if the number is not divisible by 5, and we apply the transformation, does it ever become divisible by 5? Let's check: For N not divisible by 5, f(N) = N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3. Since N is not divisible by 5, it's possible that f(N) becomes divisible by 5. For instance, 8->7, 7 not divisible by 5, but 8 has no 5, f=7 not 5. 24->26, 26 not 5. 30->31, 31 not 5. 36->39, 39 not 5. 40->38, 38 not 5. 44->37, 37 not 5. 48->52, 52 not 5. 56->50, 50 is divisible by 5. So 56 is not divisible by 5, but f(56)=50 which is divisible by 5. So if a1=56, then a2=50, which is divisible by 5. Then we need to check if 50 works? 50=2*5^2, proper divisors:1,2,5,10,25. Largest three:25,10,5 sum=40, which is divisible by 4? 40=2^3*5, then T(40)=? 40: d1=2,d2=4,d3=5? Actually 40: divisors 1,2,4,5,8,10,20,40. Smallest three:2,4,5? 2,4,5 are divisors. So T=20+10+8=38. 38->22->14->10->8->7 fails. So 56 fails. So even if a1 is not divisible by 5, the orbit may become divisible by 5, and then the sequence may fail. But our characterization of good numbers includes the condition that 5 does not divide the number. But if a1 is good, it must be that the entire orbit never hits a number divisible by 5. So the condition that 5 does not divide a1 is necessary but not sufficient? For example, 56 is not divisible by 5 but leads to 50 which is divisible by 5, and then fails. So for a1 to be good, we need that the orbit never hits a number with 5. So we need to find all a1 such that the orbit avoids numbers divisible by 5. That's a stronger condition than just a1 not divisible by 5.

But we have already derived that if a1 satisfies v2 odd and v3 ≥ (v2+1)/2, and also 5∤a1, then the orbit will eventually reach a fixed point. But does it ever become divisible by 5? Now, we need to present the proof. We'll break it into lemmas.

Lemma 1: For a composite integer N with at least three proper divisors, let d1<d2<d3 be the three smallest divisors of N (including N if necessary). Then the three largest proper divisors of N are N/d1, N/d2, N/d3, and the sum is T(N)=N/d1+N/d2+N/d3. Moreover, if N is a prime or a prime square, then it has fewer than three proper divisors, so T(N) is not defined.

Proof: Standard.

Lemma 2: The only solution in positive integers to 1/a+1/b+1/c=1 with a<b<c is (a,b,c)=(2,3,6). (Proof: Suppose a=2, then 1/b+1/c=1/2, and the only integer solution with b>2 is (3,6). If a=3, then 1/b+1/c=2/3, and b≥3 gives b=3,c=3 but then a=b, not allowed. If a=1, then 1/b+1/c=0 impossible. So only (2,3,6).)

Lemma 3: The fixed points of T are exactly numbers N such that N is divisible by 6, not divisible by 4, and not divisible by 5. (Proof: T(N)=N implies 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3=1, so (d1,d2,d3)=(2,3,6). Hence N must be divisible by 2 and 3, so N is a multiple of 6. Since d3=6, we need that 4 does not divide N (otherwise 4 would be a divisor less than 6) and 5 does not divide N (otherwise 5 would be a divisor less than 6). So v2(N)=1, 3|N, and 5∤N.)

Thus for any N not divisible by 3, T(N) < N.

Case II: 3|N. Then we have subcases based on v2(N).

- If 5∤N: then d3=6, so T(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = N, so N is a fixed point.

Thus for v2(N)=1 and 5∤N, N is a fixed point.

Subcase IIb: v2(N)≥2 and even. Then N is divisible by 4. The smallest divisors are 2,3,4 (since 4 divides N, and 3 divides N). So T(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4 = (13/12)N > N. So T(N) > N.

Subcase IIc: v2(N)≥3 and odd. Then N is divisible by 8. The smallest divisors are 2,3,4 (since 4 divides N). So T(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4 = (13/12)N > N. So T(N) > N.

Thus when 3|N and v2(N)≥2, we have T(N) > N.

Now, we can analyze the orbit.

Define S = { N : 5 ∤ N and there exists integer k≥0 such that 2^{2k+1} * 3^{k+1} | N }. This is the set of numbers we claim are good.

Proof that if N ∈ S, then the sequence is infinite.

From the formulas, we have:

- If N is not divisible by 3, then T(N) < N. So the sequence is decreasing. But we saw 56->50, and then 50->40->38->22->14->10->8->7 fails. So it doesn't reach a fixed point. So we need to show that if N is not in S, then the orbit either decreases to a prime/prime square or increases to a number that eventually becomes a fixed point? Actually if N is not in S, it could be that N is divisible by 3 with v2 even, then T(N)>N, so the sequence increases. It will then reduce the exponent of 2 by 2 each step, eventually reaching a number with v2=1. That number will be a multiple of 9? Since f≥1? If the number has v2=1 and is divisible by 3, then it's a fixed point (if 5∤N) or if it's divisible by 5, then it might not be fixed. But if N is not in S, it could be that after increasing steps, we get to a number with v2=1 and v3≥1, but if that number is also divisible by 5, then it's not a fixed point. Then what happens? That number would be of the form 2*3*t with t odd and 5|t. Then T(2*3*t) = (31/30)*2*3*t = (31/15)*t, which is not integer if t not divisible by 15? Actually we need to compute: N=6*t, t odd and 5|t. Then d1=2,d2=3,d3=5 (since 5|t, and 5<6). So T(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5 = 3t+2t+(6t/5)? Wait N=6t, so N/2=3t, N/3=2t, N/5=6t/5. So T(N)=5t+6t/5 = (25t+6t)/5=31t/5. For this to be integer, t must be divisible by 5, which it is, so T(N)=31t/5. Since t is divisible by 5, say t=5s, then N=30s, and T(N)=31s. So T(N)=31s. Now, note that N=30s, with s odd (since t odd). Then T(N)=31s. Now, 31s is not divisible by 2? It's odd. So it's not in the increasing chain. Then we need to see if 31s has at least three proper divisors. If s=1, then 31 is prime, fails. If s>1, then 31s may be composite. But also note that 31s might be divisible by 3? If s is multiple of 3, then 31s divisible by 3, but then the transformation might go back to the increasing chain. So it's complex.

Thus to show that if N ∉ S, then the sequence cannot be infinite, we need a more careful argument.

Given the time, we can adopt a different approach: Show that the only numbers that can be in the basin of attraction of the fixed points are those that satisfy the condition. This can be done by considering the process as a "reduction" to a fixed point. We can prove that if N is such that the orbit is infinite, then N must be in S. Conversely, S works. So the answer is S.

We can prove the necessity by induction on the size of N, or by analyzing the exponents.

Observation: The transformation T has the property that if N is not a fixed point, then either T(N) < N (if 3∤N) or T(N) > N (if 3|N and v2≥2). So the sequence is monotonic. If it is decreasing, it will eventually reach a number that is either a fixed point or a prime/prime square. Since it's infinite, it must reach a fixed point. So there exists some m such that T^m(N) is a fixed point. Now, if T^m(N) is a fixed point, then T^m(N) ∈ S. We can then try to show that N must also be in S. This is because the transformation T, when applied to numbers with v2≥2, reduces v2 by 2 and v3 by 1, and also multiplies the odd part by 13. So if we start from a fixed point (v2=1, v3≥1, no 5), and apply the inverse of T (which is not unique), we can generate numbers that are in S. But we need to show that if N is not in S, then T^m(N) cannot be a fixed point. This is plausible because the condition on valuations is preserved under the transformation? Actually if N is not in S, then either v2(N) is even (and ≥2) or v3(N) < (v2(N)+1)/2. Under T, if v2(N)≥2 and 3|N, then T(N) = 13N/12, so v2' = v2-2, v3' = v3-1. So the "deficit" (v3 - (v2+1)/2) changes. Let's define Δ(N) = v3(N) - (v2(N)+1)/2. Then for N with v2 odd and v3≥(v2+1)/2, we have Δ≥0. Under T (when 3|N and v2≥2), v2' = v2-2, v3' = v3-1, so Δ' = (v3-1) - ((v2-2)+1)/2 = v3-1 - (v2-1)/2 = v3 - (v2+1)/2 = Δ. So Δ is invariant under the increasing step. So if Δ(N) ≥ 0, then all subsequent numbers in the increasing chain also have Δ ≥ 0. But if Δ(N) < 0, then after one step, Δ' = v3-1 - (v2-1)/2 = v3 - (v2+1)/2 = Δ, so it remains negative. So the condition Δ < 0 is preserved. So if N has Δ < 0, then the increasing chain will always have Δ < 0. But when v2 becomes 1, then the condition for fixed point requires Δ ≥ 0? Actually if v2=1, then (v2+1)/2=1, so Δ = v3-1. For a fixed point, we need v3≥1, so Δ ≥ 0. So if Δ < 0, then when we reach v2=1, we have v3=0? Because Δ = v3-1 <0 implies v3=0. So the number would be of the form 2*t, with t odd and not divisible by 3. Then T(2t) < 2t, and the sequence decreases. So if we ever get to a number with v2=1 and v3=0, then the next step will decrease. And that decrease may lead to a prime or prime square. So to have an infinite orbit, we must avoid that. So it's necessary that Δ(N) ≥ 0. Also we must avoid 5 dividing any number in the orbit. But as we saw, if 5 divides a number, then the sequence may become undefined. So we need 5∤N. Thus N must satisfy Δ(N) ≥ 0 and 5∤N. But Δ(N) ≥ 0 means v3(N) ≥ (v2(N)+1)/2. And v2(N) must be odd? Not necessarily: if v2(N) is even, then (v2+1)/2 is not an integer, but the inequality v3 ≥ (v2+1)/2 is a real inequality. For v2 even, say v2=2, then (v2+1)/2=1.5, so v3≥1.5 means v3≥2? However, we have seen that 36 fails. So the condition Δ≥0 is not sufficient; we also need v2 odd? If v2 even, say 2, then after one step, v2'=0, which is even, and then the number becomes a power of 2? Actually if v2=2 and 3|N, then T(N)=13N/12. So the condition for a fixed point is v2=1 and v3≥1. So if we ever get to a number with v2=1 and v3≥1, it's a fixed point. That number must be in S. So if N is not in S, then the orbit cannot hit a fixed point because that would imply that after some steps, we get to a number with v2=1 and v3≥1, which is in S. But that doesn't preclude N from being in S. So the necessity is: If the orbit is infinite, then eventually it must hit a fixed point (since if it is decreasing, it will become prime/prime square unless it hits a fixed point; if it is increasing, it will reduce v2 until it reaches 1, and then if v3≥1, it's a fixed point; if v3=0, it's not a fixed point and then the sequence will decrease). So if the orbit is infinite, it must hit a fixed point. Hence there exists m such that T^m(N) is a fixed point. So T^m(N) ∈ S. Now, we need to show that N itself must be in S.
